rel=\"nofollow\"\u003ehttps://docs.python.org/3/library/functions.html\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSome functions we use a lot in 220/320 are abs, dir, float, input, int, len, list, max, min, range, set, sorted, str, sum, type.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e7 and 2 are ints, so the result of dividing these is an int (3, after rounding down 3.5) in most programming languages.  Python produces the mathematically correct answer, even though it is not an int (like 7 and 2).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn other cases where you want to divide 7 by 2 and get an int, you would use \u003ccode\u003e7 // 2\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIMPORTANT\u003c/strong\u003e: After solving every question, we recommend running the tester, to confirm whether your code is able to clear the auto-grader tests locally on your VM. \u003ccode\u003e[item1, item2, ...]\u003c/code\u003e and indexed/sliced just like strings (strings and lists are both examples of Python \u003cem\u003esequences\u003c/em\u003e; by definition, you can index and slice any kind of sequence you encounter in Python).  This list contains just ints, but you're free to have a mix of types in Python lists.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn general, you can plug in a variable name and sequence into a \u003ccode\u003efor\u003c/code\u003e loop to run a piece of code for every entry in the sequence:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"for ???? in ????:\n    # DO SOMETHING\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003efor\u003c/span\u003e ???? \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ein\u003c/span\u003e ????:\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e# DO SOMETHING\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eMore on \u003ccode\u003efor\u003c/code\u003e loops:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#for-statements\" rel=\"nofollow\"\u003ehttps://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#for-statements\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#break-and-continue-statements-and-else-clauses-on-loops\" rel=\"nofollow\"\u003ehttps://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#break-and-continue-statements-and-else-clauses-on-loops\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWrite a function called \u003ccode\u003esmart_count\u003c/code\u003e that takes a list of numbers and returns their sum.  It should also have the following features:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eignore numbers greater than 10\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eif there is a negative number, that number (and all that follow it, positive or negative, should be skipped)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNote\u003c/strong\u003e: if you try to use \u003ccode\u003esum\u003c/code\u003e as the variable to keep track of the sum, notice how it turns green. Anything that turns green inside notebook file format is a Python keyword, meaning you shouldn't be using that as a variable. Can you think of a better variable name than \u003ccode\u003esum\u003c/code\u003e? Learn this skill early so that you don't accidentally redefine the meaning of the keywords that already have a different meaning.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eUse \u003ccode\u003econtinue\u003c/code\u003e to implement feature 1 and \u003ccode\u003ebreak\u003c/code\u003e to implement feature 2.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ca \u003ccode\u003erows\u003c/code\u003e is a list of lists. With lists, each value is associated with an index (integers starting from 0).  With dicts, each value is associated with a key specified by the programmers. Note that these two dicts are considered equivalent since they have the same values corresponding to the same keys.  So if the tester complains about any of your dicts (or sets) being wrong, the issue is with the contents, not the order.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ca yearly, asking a variety of questions.  The following gives data on household computer use from the years 2013 to 2018:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://data.census.gov/cedsci/table?t=Computer%20and%20Internet%20Use\u0026amp;g=0100000US%240400000\u0026amp;tid=ACSDT1Y2015.B28001\u0026amp;hidePreview=true\u0026amp;tp=true\u0026amp;moe=true\" rel=\"nofollow\"\u003ehttps://data.census.gov/cedsci/table?t=Computer%20and%20Internet%20Use\u0026amp;g=0100000US%240400000\u0026amp;tid=ACSDT1Y2015.B28001\u0026amp;hidePreview=true\u0026amp;tp=true\u0026amp;moe=true\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWe have downloaded the data for each year to a file in the \u003ccode\u003ehome-computers\u003c/code\u003e directory.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCreate a dictionary called \u003ccode\u003eyears\u003c/code\u003e like this:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ekey\u003c/strong\u003e: a year (int), corresponding to a year of data in the directory.  Don't hardcode the years -- use \u003ccode\u003eos.listdir\u003c/code\u003e and extract the year from each filename (right before the first \u003ccode\u003e.\u003c/code\u003e). Check if the file is a CSV by checking if it ends with \".csv\". Don't hardcode the years in the dictionary - use the year extracted from the file name as the key.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003evalue\u003c/strong\u003e: a pandas DataFrame corresponding to the CSV for that year.  Skip the first row from each CSV file: \u003ca href=\"https://pandas.pydata.org/pandas-docs/dev/reference/api/pandas.read_csv.html\" rel=\"nofollow\"\u003ehttps://pandas.pydata.org/pandas-docs/dev/reference/api/pandas.read_csv.html\u003c/a\u003e.  Use \u003ccode\u003eset_index\u003c/code\u003e (\u003ca href=\"https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/reference/api/pandas.DataFrame.set_index.html\" rel=\"nofollow\"\u003ehttps://pandas.pydata.org/docs/reference/api/pandas.DataFrame.set_index.html\u003c/a\u003e) to make \"Geographic Area Name\" the index of the DataFrame.  This will let you easily look up state stats by name (instead of by row number) later.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ca (2018)\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe data is in the \"Estimate!!Total!!No Computer\" column.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIf \u003ccode\u003edf\u003c/code\u003e is a DataFrame, \u003ccode\u003edf[\"some column name here\"]\u003c/code\u003e will extract an individual column as a Pandas Series.  A Pandas Series is like a list/dict hybrid.  You can use \u003ccode\u003e.iat\u003c/code\u003e to look up values by integer position (like you would with a list).  You can use \u003ccode\u003e.at\u003c/code\u003e to look up values by the Series' index, like you would with a dict.  Note the confusing terminology here: a Series' index is like a dict's key, and the \"i\" in \"iat\" does NOT refer to \"index\".\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIf you have a Pandas Series \u003ccode\u003es\u003c/code\u003e, you can do various aggregations on it, like \u003ccode\u003e.mean()\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003e.sum()\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003e.max()\u003c/code\u003e, etc.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-q20-what-is-the-biggest-per-state-margin-of-error-for-no-computer-as-a-fraction-of-the-total-estimate-2018\" class=\"anchor\" aria-hidden=\"true\" tabindex=\"-1\" href=\"#q20-what-is-the-biggest-per-state-margin-of-error-for-no-computer-as-a-fraction-of-the-total-estimate-2018\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003eQ20: what is the biggest per-state margin of error for \"No Computer\", as a fraction of the total estimate? <h2 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-overview" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#overview"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Overview</h2>
<p dir="auto">This project is longer than usual, with 32 questions, because it is
designed to get familiar with Python basics (or review them if you
took 220).  The first 75% can be done as a group and focuses on
review.  The last 25% is individual and focuses on new 320 concepts.</p>
<p dir="auto">Learning objectives:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>write code to control the flow of execution with loops, functions, and if statements</li>
<li>extract values from Python state (variables, data structures, etc), including nested list and dict structures</li>
<li>use the <code>pandas</code> package to read CSV files into DataFrames and analyze/plot the resulting data</li>
<li>write code to analyze the history of a git project</li>
<li>measure performance (how long it takes to run things)</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Note:</strong> some browser's have a "dark mode" -- be careful not to use it when looking at plot examples (it often hides axis labels in the examples, which you need for full credit).</p>
<h2 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-setup" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#setup"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Setup</h2>

virtual machine.</p>

(<a href="https://console.cloud.google.com/compute/metadata/sshKeys" rel="nofollow">https://console.cloud.google.com/compute/metadata/sshKeys</a>) and
External IP address
(<a href="https://console.cloud.google.com/compute/instances" rel="nofollow">https://console.cloud.google.com/compute/instances</a>) in Google's
console.</p>
<ol start="2" dir="auto">
<li>

</li>

</li>

</li>
</ol>

<p dir="auto"><code>nohup python3 -m jupyterlab --no-browser --ip=0.0.0.0 --port=2020 &amp;</code></p>
<ol start="5" dir="auto">

</li>

</li>

<h2 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-python-control-flow-part-1" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#python-control-flow-part-1"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Python Control Flow (Part 1)</h2>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q1-what-is-the-type-of-72" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q1-what-is-the-type-of-72"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q1: what is the type of <code>7/2</code>?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Take a look at the builtin Python functions to see if one can answer this: <a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/functions.html" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/library/functions.html</a></p>
<p dir="auto">Some functions we use a lot in 220/320 are abs, dir, float, input, int, len, list, max, min, range, set, sorted, str, sum, type.</p>
<p dir="auto">7 and 2 are ints, so the result of dividing these is an int (3, after rounding down 3.5) in most programming languages.  Python produces the mathematically correct answer, even though it is not an int (like 7 and 2).</p>
<p dir="auto">In other cases where you want to divide 7 by 2 and get an int, you would use <code>7 // 2</code>.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>IMPORTANT</strong>: After solving every question, we recommend running the tester, to confirm whether your code is able to clear the auto-grader tests locally on your VM. For example, after solving q1, if you run the tester, you will see the output will look similar to the below image:</p>
<p dir="auto"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="/msyamkumar/cs320-s23-projects/blob/main/p1/img/tester.png"><img src="/msyamkumar/cs320-s23-projects/raw/main/p1/img/tester.png" style="max-width: 100%;"></a></p>
<p dir="auto">Please keep in mind that even though your code might clear the tester on your local VM, it might fail the tester on the submission portal. It is your responsibility to check that your submission clears auto-grader on the submission portal (course website). You will receive a canvas message when the auto-grader test results are ready - <strong>PLEASE DO NOT IGNORE</strong> THAT and make sure to check your auto-grader test results on time.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q2-what-is-error" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q2-what-is-error"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q2: what is <code>error</code>?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Complete the code in accordance with the comment to calculate the answer.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="x = 4
maximum = 10
minimum = 5
error = ???? # True if x is outside the minimum-to-maximum range
error"><pre><span class="pl-s1">x</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">4</span>
<span class="pl-s1">maximum</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">10</span>
<span class="pl-s1">minimum</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">5</span>
<span class="pl-s1">error</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> ???? <span class="pl-c"># True if x is outside the minimum-to-maximum range</span>
<span class="pl-s1">error</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Notes:</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li>we don't need to specify the type of our variables as in some languages (e.g., Java) -- Python knows x is an <code>int</code> because we assigned <code>4</code>, which is an int.  Variable types are not fixed after creation as in some languages (e.g., Go) -- we could later run <code>x = "howdy"</code> if we wanted to</li>
<li>in Python, a <code>bool</code> is <code>True</code> or <code>False</code>.  We use the <code>and</code>, <code>or</code>, and <code>not</code> operators (in other programming languages, these operators are often expressed as <code>&amp;&amp;</code>, <code>||</code>, and <code>!</code>).</li>
</ol>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q3-ignoring-case-does-word-end-with-the-suffix-esque" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q3-ignoring-case-does-word-end-with-the-suffix-esque"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q3: ignoring case, does <code>word</code> end with the suffix "esque"?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Complete the following to answer:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="word = &quot;KAFKAESQUE&quot;
suffix_match = ???? # .endswith(...) method not allowed for this question! (practice slicing)

<span class="pl-s1">suffix_match</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Skim string methods here: <a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html#string-methods</a>.  Some important ones: <code>find</code>, <code>isdigit</code>, <code>join</code>, <code>split</code>, <code>lower</code>, <code>upper</code>, <code>strip</code>, <code>replace</code>.</p>
<p dir="auto">Hints:</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li>to ignore case, it's often easy to use a method to make everything upper or lower case</li>
<li>to get a single character from a string, you can use <code>s[INDEX]</code>.  0 is the first character, 1 is the second, and so on.  Python supports negative indexing, meaning <code>s[-1]</code> is the last letter, <code>s[-2]</code> is the next to last, etc.  You can also <em>slice</em> strings to get a substring by putting a colon between two indexes <code>s[inclusive_start:exclusive_end]</code>.  You can leave off one of the indexes to go to the start or end of the string.  For example, <code>word[:3]</code> would evaluate to <code>"KAF"</code>.</li>
<li>in Java, you compare strings with <code>s1.equals(s2)</code>, but in Python the correct equivalent is <code>s1 == s2</code>.  The equivalent of Java's <code>==</code> is Python's rarely used <code>is</code> operator.</li>
</ol>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-requirement-add-function" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#requirement-add-function"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Requirement <code>add</code> function:</h3>
<p dir="auto">Your function should generally take two ints and return their sum.  For example, <code>add(2, 3)</code> should return 5.  Users of the function should also be able to call it like <code>add(x=2, y=3)</code>.  If only one argument is passed, 1 should be added.  For example, <code>add(3)</code> or <code>add(x=3)</code> would both return 4.</p>
<p dir="auto">Python parameters may be filled with positions arguments, keyword arguments, or default arguments.  If this is unfamiliar, read the following:</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#defining-functions" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#defining-functions</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#more-on-defining-functions" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#more-on-defining-functions</a></li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">In Python, indents are very important.  The code inside a function/if/loop is indented (Python doesn't use <code>{</code> and <code>{</code> to indicate this, as in Java and many other languages).</p>

<p dir="auto">Python lists can be created like <code>[item1, item2, ...]</code> and indexed/sliced just like strings (strings and lists are both examples of Python <em>sequences</em>; by definition, you can index and slice any kind of sequence you encounter in Python).  This list contains just ints, but you're free to have a mix of types in Python lists.</p>
<p dir="auto">In general, you can plug in a variable name and sequence into a <code>for</code> loop to run a piece of code for every entry in the sequence:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="for ???? in ????:
    # DO SOMETHING"><pre><span class="pl-k">for</span> ???? <span class="pl-c1">in</span> ????:

<p dir="auto">More on <code>for</code> loops:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#for-statements" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#for-statements</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#break-and-continue-statements-and-else-clauses-on-loops" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/controlflow.html#break-and-continue-statements-and-else-clauses-on-loops</a></li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Write a function called <code>smart_count</code> that takes a list of numbers and returns their sum.  It should also have the following features:</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li>ignore numbers greater than 10</li>
<li>if there is a negative number, that number (and all that follow it, positive or negative, should be skipped)</li>
<li><strong>Note</strong>: if you try to use <code>sum</code> as the variable to keep track of the sum, notice how it turns green. Anything that turns green inside notebook file format is a Python keyword, meaning you shouldn't be using that as a variable. Can you think of a better variable name than <code>sum</code>? Learn this skill early so that you don't accidentally redefine the meaning of the keywords that already have a different meaning.</li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">Use <code>continue</code> to implement feature 1 and <code>break</code> to implement feature 2.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q7-what-is-smart_countnums" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q7-what-is-smart_countnums"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q7: what is <code>smart_count(nums)</code>?</h3>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q8-what-is-smart_count2-1-11-3-15--1-8-2" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q8-what-is-smart_count2-1-11-3-15--1-8-2"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q8: what is <code>smart_count([2, 1, 11, 3, 15, -1, 8, 2])</code>?</h3>
<p dir="auto">The answer should be 6: 2+1+3.  11 and 15 are too large, so they are skipped.  8 and 2 are skipped because they are after a negative number (which is also skipped).</p>
<h2 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-python-state-part-2" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#python-state-part-2"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Python State (Part 2)</h2>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-requirement-lists-and-dicts" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#requirement-lists-and-dicts"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Requirement: lists and dicts</h3>
<p dir="auto">Copy/paste the following:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="header = [&quot;A&quot;, &quot;B&quot;, &quot;C&quot;]

<p dir="auto">Note that <code>rows</code> is a list of lists.  Each inner list contains two ints and one dict (dictionary).  For complicated nested structures like this, it's often helpful to visualize the stack of frames and heap of objects in PythonTutor: <a href="https://pythontutor.com/live.html#mode=edit" rel="nofollow">https://pythontutor.com/live.html#mode=edit</a>.</p>
<p dir="auto">You could copy the above to visualize it, or use the following link for your convenience:</p>
<p dir="auto"><a href="https://pythontutor.com/visualize.html#code=header%20%3D%20%5B%22A%22,%20%22B%22,%20%22C%22%5D%0A%0Acoord1%20%3D%20%7B%22x%22%3A%208,%20%22y%22%3A%205%7D%0Acoord2%20%3D%20%7B%22x%22%3A%209,%20%22y%22%3A%202%7D%0Acoord3%20%3D%20%7B%22x%22%3A%203,%20%22y%22%3A%201%7D%0A%0Arows%20%3D%20%5B%0A%20%20%20%20%5B1,%206,%20coord1%5D,%0A%20%20%20%20%5B3,%204,%20coord2%5D,%0A%20%20%20%20%5B5,%202,%20coord3%5D,%0A%5D&amp;cumulative=false&amp;curInstr=7&amp;heapPrimitives=nevernest&amp;mode=display&amp;origin=opt-frontend.js&amp;py=3&amp;rawInputLstJSON=%5B%5D&amp;textReferences=false" rel="nofollow">https://pythontutor.com/visualize.html#code=header%20%3D%20%5B%22A%22,%20%22B%22,%20%22C%22%5D%0A%0Acoord1%20%3D%20%7B%22x%22%3A%208,%20%22y%22%3A%205%7D%0Acoord2%20%3D%20%7B%22x%22%3A%209,%20%22y%22%3A%202%7D%0Acoord3%20%3D%20%7B%22x%22%3A%203,%20%22y%22%3A%201%7D%0A%0Arows%20%3D%20%5B%0A%20%20%20%20%5B1,%206,%20coord1%5D,%0A%20%20%20%20%5B3,%204,%20coord2%5D,%0A%20%20%20%20%5B5,%202,%20coord3%5D,%0A%5D&amp;cumulative=false&amp;curInstr=7&amp;heapPrimitives=nevernest&amp;mode=display&amp;origin=opt-frontend.js&amp;py=3&amp;rawInputLstJSON=%5B%5D&amp;textReferences=false</a></p>
<p dir="auto">Both lists and dicts contain values.  With lists, each value is associated with an index (integers starting from 0).  With dicts, each value is associated with a key specified by the programmers.  Keys are often strings, but they don't need to be.</p>
<p dir="auto">Docs:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/datastructures.html#more-on-lists" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/datastructures.html#more-on-lists</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/datastructures.html#dictionaries" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/datastructures.html#dictionaries</a></li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Testing Note:</strong> try running <code>{"A":1, "B":2} == {"B":2, "A":1}</code> in Python.  Note that these two dicts are considered equivalent since they have the same values corresponding to the same keys.  So if the tester complains about any of your dicts (or sets) being wrong, the issue is with the contents, not the order.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q9-after-inserting-a-z-key-in-coord3-with-coord3z--314-what-is-rows" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q9-after-inserting-a-z-key-in-coord3-with-coord3z--314-what-is-rows"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q9: after inserting a "z" key in <code>coord3</code> (with <code>coord3["z"] = 3.14</code>), what is <code>rows</code>?</h3>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q10-what-is-the-value-associated-with-the-x-key-of-the-dict-in-the-last-position-of-the-first-list" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q10-what-is-the-value-associated-with-the-x-key-of-the-dict-in-the-last-position-of-the-first-list"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q10: what is the value associated with the "x" key of the dict in the last position of the first list?</h3>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Hint:</strong> if the question were "what is the value associated with the 'y' key of the dict in the last position of the second list?", the solution would be: <code>rows[1][-1]["y"]</code>.  You just need to track on brackets containing indexes (for lists) or keys (for dicts) to delve deeper into a nested structure.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q11-what-is-rows-after-running-the-following" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q11-what-is-rows-after-running-the-following"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q11: what is <code>rows</code> after running the following?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Complete the following so that the first change via <code>v2</code> is NOT reflected in <code>rows</code>, but the second change via <code>v2</code> IS reflected in <code>rows</code>:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="import copy
v2 = ????
v2[0] = 8888    # first change
v2[1][1] = 9999 # second change"><pre><span class="pl-k">import</span> <span class="pl-s1">copy</span>
<span class="pl-s1">v2</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> ????
<span class="pl-s1">v2</span>[<span class="pl-c1">0</span>] <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">8888</span>    <span class="pl-c"># first change</span>
<span class="pl-s1">v2</span>[<span class="pl-c1">1</span>][<span class="pl-c1">1</span>] <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">9999</span> <span class="pl-c"># second change</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Relevant docs: <a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/copy.html" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/library/copy.html</a></p>
<p dir="auto">To get a good intuition about the reference/shallow/deep copy, try stepping through the following slowly in PythonTutor:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="import copy
v1 = [[1], [], [2, 3]]
v2 = v1
v2 = copy.copy(v1)
v2 = copy.deepcopy(v1)"><pre><span class="pl-k">import</span> <span class="pl-s1">copy</span>
<span class="pl-s1">v1</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> [[<span class="pl-c1">1</span>], [], [<span class="pl-c1">2</span>, <span class="pl-c1">3</span>]]
<span class="pl-s1">v2</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s1">v1</span>
<span class="pl-s1">v2</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s1">copy</span>.<span class="pl-en">copy</span>(<span class="pl-s1">v1</span>)
<span class="pl-s1">v2</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s1">copy</span>.<span class="pl-en">deepcopy</span>(<span class="pl-s1">v1</span>)</pre></div>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q12-if-we-imagine-the-list-of-lists-structure-referenced-by-rows-as-a-table-with-column-names-in-header-what-is-the-sum-of-values-in-the-b-column" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q12-if-we-imagine-the-list-of-lists-structure-referenced-by-rows-as-a-table-with-column-names-in-header-what-is-the-sum-of-values-in-the-b-column"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q12: if we imagine the list of lists structure referenced by <code>rows</code> as a table, with column names in <code>header</code>, what is the sum of values in the "B" column?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Note: the "B" column corresponds to the values at index in 1 of each list, but you are not allowed to hardcode 1 for this solution.  Instead, use <code>header.index(????)</code> to look up the position of "B" within the <code>header</code> list.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q13-what-is-rows-after-we-sort-it-in-place-by-the-b-column-ascending" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q13-what-is-rows-after-we-sort-it-in-place-by-the-b-column-ascending"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q13: what is <code>rows</code> after we sort it in-place by the "B" column, ascending?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Docs:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/howto/sorting.html#sorting-basics" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/howto/sorting.html#sorting-basics</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/howto/sorting.html#key-functions" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/howto/sorting.html#key-functions</a></li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Hint: if we had to sort by the "A" column <strong>descending</strong>, we could do the following:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def get_column_a(row):
    print(&quot;lookup A column for a row&quot;)
    return row[header.index(&quot;A&quot;)]

rows.sort(key=get_column_a, reverse=True)
rows"><pre><span class="pl-k">def</span> <span class="pl-en">get_column_a</span>(<span class="pl-s1">row</span>):
    <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">"lookup A column for a row"</span>)
    <span class="pl-k">return</span> <span class="pl-s1">row</span>[<span class="pl-s1">header</span>.<span class="pl-en">index</span>(<span class="pl-s">"A"</span>)]

<span class="pl-s1">rows</span>.<span class="pl-en">sort</span>(<span class="pl-s1">key</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span><span class="pl-s1">get_column_a</span>, <span class="pl-s1">reverse</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span><span class="pl-c1">True</span>)
<span class="pl-s1">rows</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Note that we aren't calling <code>get_column_a</code> ourselves (because there are no parentheses after it on the sort line).  Instead, we're giving the <code>sort</code> method a reference to that function; this allows <code>sort</code> to call the function on each row, to figure out what part of the row objects matters for the sort.</p>
<p dir="auto">When we only need a function for one purpose, we can use the <code>lambda</code> syntax instead of the <code>def</code> syntax to define the function on a single line, without even giving it a name.  The following works the same as the earlier example (but without the print):</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="rows.sort(key=lambda row: row[header.index(&quot;A&quot;)], reverse=True)
rows"><pre><span class="pl-s1">rows</span>.<span class="pl-en">sort</span>(<span class="pl-s1">key</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span><span class="pl-k">lambda</span> <span class="pl-s1">row</span>: <span class="pl-s1">row</span>[<span class="pl-s1">header</span>.<span class="pl-en">index</span>(<span class="pl-s">"A"</span>)], <span class="pl-s1">reverse</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span><span class="pl-c1">True</span>)
<span class="pl-s1">rows</span></pre></div>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q14-say-youre-going-on-vacation-to-europe-with-400-us-dollars-how-many-euros-can-you-get-at-the-current-exchange-rate" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q14-say-youre-going-on-vacation-to-europe-with-400-us-dollars-how-many-euros-can-you-get-at-the-current-exchange-rate"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q14: say you're going on vacation to Europe with 400 US dollars; how many Euros can you get at the current exchange rate?</h3>
<p dir="auto">This site provides exchange rate information in JSON format: <a href="https://www.floatrates.com/json-feeds.html" rel="nofollow">https://www.floatrates.com/json-feeds.html</a>.  JSON is a simple format that can represent nested dicts and lists in files and web resources.</p>
<p dir="auto">Download a copy of <code>usd.json</code> to the directory where your project is.  An easy way is to open a terminal, <code>cd</code> to the appriate directory, then run <code>wget SOME_URL_HERE</code> to download the web resource.</p>
<p dir="auto">Note: you can run shell commands in Jupyter, too, if you start the command with a <code>!</code> (to indicate it is not Python code).  If you do this, be sure to delete the cell after the download.  Otherwise you'll create too much traffic on the floatrates.com site, re-downloading the same thing every time you re-run your notebook.</p>
<p dir="auto">You can read a file like this:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="f = open(&quot;usd.json&quot;)
data = f.read()
f.close()"><pre><span class="pl-s1">f</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-en">open</span>(<span class="pl-s">"usd.json"</span>)
<span class="pl-s1">data</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s1">f</span>.<span class="pl-en">read</span>()
<span class="pl-s1">f</span>.<span class="pl-en">close</span>()</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Check the type of <code>data</code> and the first portion of it:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="print(type(data))
print(data[:300] + &quot;...&quot;)"><pre><span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-en">type</span>(<span class="pl-s1">data</span>))
<span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s1">data</span>[:<span class="pl-c1">300</span>] <span class="pl-c1">+</span> <span class="pl-s">"..."</span>)</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Even though the file contains a string that <em>could</em> be interpreted as JSON, Python won't <em>deserialize</em> it to Python dicts/lists automatically.  Instead of calling <code>.read()</code>, we need to use the <code>load</code> function in the <code>json</code> module:</p>
<p dir="auto"><a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/json.html#json.load" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/library/json.html#json.load</a></p>
<p dir="auto">When reading documentation, start by focusing on parameters that can't take default arguments.</p>
<p dir="auto">Access the exchange rate for Euros in the dictionary and multiply it by the amount of US dollars you have to find out how many Euros you can get.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-requirement-divide-function" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#requirement-divide-function"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Requirement: <code>divide</code> function</h3>
<p dir="auto">Normally, if you divide by 0, you'll get an exception.  Write a function that does division; when there is such an exception, is should catch it and return the float <code>nan</code> (not a number).</p>
<p dir="auto">How to catch exceptions: <a href="https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/errors.html#handling-exceptions" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/errors.html#handling-exceptions</a></p>
<p dir="auto">To get <code>nan</code>, you can convert a string: <code>float("nan")</code></p>
<p dir="auto">Requirement: the function should only catch the exception that gets thrown for division by zero (not other exceptions).  To find the name of this exception, you could try doing a simple division by zero in a cell and observe what gets thrown.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q15-what-is-divide3-2" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q15-what-is-divide3-2"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q15: what is <code>divide(3, 2)</code>?</h3>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q16-what-is-divide-3-0" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q16-what-is-divide-3-0"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q16: what is <code>divide(-3, 0)</code>?</h3>
<h2 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-python-data-science-part-3" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#python-data-science-part-3"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Python Data Science (Part 3)</h2>
<p dir="auto">The US Census Bureau conducts the ACS (American Community Survey) yearly, asking a variety of questions.  The following gives data on household computer use from the years 2013 to 2018:</p>
<p dir="auto"><a href="https://data.census.gov/cedsci/table?t=Computer%20and%20Internet%20Use&amp;g=0100000US%240400000&amp;tid=ACSDT1Y2015.B28001&amp;hidePreview=true&amp;tp=true&amp;moe=true" rel="nofollow">https://data.census.gov/cedsci/table?t=Computer%20and%20Internet%20Use&amp;g=0100000US%240400000&amp;tid=ACSDT1Y2015.B28001&amp;hidePreview=true&amp;tp=true&amp;moe=true</a></p>
<p dir="auto">We have downloaded the data for each year to a file in the <code>home-computers</code> directory.</p>
<p dir="auto">Create a dictionary called <code>years</code> like this:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><strong>key</strong>: a year (int), corresponding to a year of data in the directory.  Don't hardcode the years -- use <code>os.listdir</code> and extract the year from each filename (right before the first <code>.</code>). Check if the file is a CSV by checking if it ends with ".csv". Don't hardcode the years in the dictionary - use the year extracted from the file name as the key.</li>
<li><strong>value</strong>: a pandas DataFrame corresponding to the CSV for that year.  Skip the first row from each CSV file: <a href="https://pandas.pydata.org/pandas-docs/dev/reference/api/pandas.read_csv.html" rel="nofollow">https://pandas.pydata.org/pandas-docs/dev/reference/api/pandas.read_csv.html</a>.  Use <code>set_index</code> (<a href="https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/reference/api/pandas.DataFrame.set_index.html" rel="nofollow">https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/reference/api/pandas.DataFrame.set_index.html</a>) to make "Geographic Area Name" the index of the DataFrame.  This will let you easily look up state stats by name (instead of by row number) later.</li>
</ul>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q17-what-are-the-keys-in-years" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q17-what-are-the-keys-in-years"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q17: what are the keys in <code>years</code>?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Answer with a sorted list.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q18-how-many-households-did-wisconsin-have-in-2018" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q18-how-many-households-did-wisconsin-have-in-2018"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q18: how many households did Wisconsin have in 2018?</h3>
<p dir="auto">The answer is in row 49 and column 1.  The hardcoding way to answer (not allowed) would thus be this:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="df = years[2018]
df.iat[49, 1] # iat works like df.iloc[49, 1], but is faster for one cell"><pre><span class="pl-s1">df</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s1">years</span>[<span class="pl-c1">2018</span>]
<span class="pl-s1">df</span>.<span class="pl-s1">iat</span>[<span class="pl-c1">49</span>, <span class="pl-c1">1</span>] <span class="pl-c"># iat works like df.iloc[49, 1], but is faster for one cell</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Instead of hardcoding 49 and 1, you can use "Wisconsin" (row index name) and "Estimate!!Total" (column name).  When using names instead of positions, you just need to use <code>.at</code> or <code>.loc</code> (instead of <code>.iat</code>).</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q19-how-many-total-households-in-the-us-are-estimated-to-not-have-any-computer-at-home-2018" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q19-how-many-total-households-in-the-us-are-estimated-to-not-have-any-computer-at-home-2018"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q19: how many total households in the US are estimated to not have any computer at home? (2018)</h3>
<p dir="auto">The data is in the "Estimate!!Total!!No Computer" column.</p>
<p dir="auto">If <code>df</code> is a DataFrame, <code>df["some column name here"]</code> will extract an individual column as a Pandas Series.  A Pandas Series is like a list/dict hybrid.  You can use <code>.iat</code> to look up values by integer position (like you would with a list).  You can use <code>.at</code> to look up values by the Series' index, like you would with a dict.  Note the confusing terminology here: a Series' index is like a dict's key, and the "i" in "iat" does NOT refer to "index".</p>
<p dir="auto">If you have a Pandas Series <code>s</code>, you can do various aggregations on it, like <code>.mean()</code>, <code>.sum()</code>, <code>.max()</code>, etc.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q20-what-is-the-biggest-per-state-margin-of-error-for-no-computer-as-a-fraction-of-the-total-estimate-2018" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q20-what-is-the-biggest-per-state-margin-of-error-for-no-computer-as-a-fraction-of-the-total-estimate-2018"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q20: what is the biggest per-state margin of error for "No Computer", as a fraction of the total estimate? (2018)</h3>
<p dir="auto">The margin of error is given in the "Margin of Error!!Total!!No Computer" column.</p>
<p dir="auto">You can divide one Pandas Series by another on an elementwise basis like this: <code>s3 = s2 / s1</code>.  You can then compute <code>s3.max()</code>.  Or better, see if you can combine everything into a one-line computation.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q21-for-wisconsin-and-adjacent-states-what-percent-of-households-are-estimated-to-be-without-a-computer-2018" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q21-for-wisconsin-and-adjacent-states-what-percent-of-households-are-estimated-to-be-without-a-computer-2018"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q21: for Wisconsin and adjacent states, what percent of households are estimated to be without a computer? (2018)</h3>
<p dir="auto"><strong>States</strong>: Illinois, Indiana, Iowa, Michigan, Minnesota, Wisconsin.</p>
<p dir="auto">Use the "Estimate!!Total!!No Computer" and "Estimate!!Total" columns to calculate the percentage of households without a computer.</p>

<h1 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-individual-part-25" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#individual-part-25"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Individual Part (25%)</h1>
<p dir="auto">You have to do the remainder of this project on your own.  Do not
discuss with anybody except 320 staff (mentors, TAs, instructor).</p>
<p dir="auto">For this part, you'll do two things:</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li>analyze the history of this project: <a href="https://github.com/pallets/flask">https://github.com/pallets/flask</a>.  We'll eventually learn how to use the flask module to build web applications -- for now we'll just analyze changes to the codebase over time.</li>
<li>measure how long various Pandas operations take</li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">We have a copy of the flask repo in <code>flask.zip</code>.  Run <code>unzip flask.zip</code>.  If <code>unzip</code> is not installed, follow the suggestion in the error message to install it.  If that doesn't work because you don't have admin permissions, re-run the suggested command with <code>sudo</code> in front of the suggestion (that runs the command as the super/root/admin user).</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q25-what-is-the-first-line-of-output-from-git-log-when-run-in-the-flask-repo-directory" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q25-what-is-the-first-line-of-output-from-git-log-when-run-in-the-flask-repo-directory"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q25: what is the first line of output from <code>git log</code> when run in the <code>flask</code> repo directory?</h3>
<p dir="auto">If you pass <code>cwd="????"</code> to <code>check_output</code>, you can run the <code>git log</code> command inside the <code>flask</code> directory that was created when you ran the <code>git clone</code> command.  "CWD" stands for "change working directory".</p>
<p dir="auto"><code>check_output</code> function in the <code>subprocess</code> module (<a href="https://docs.python.org/3.10/library/subprocess.html#subprocess.check_output" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3.10/library/subprocess.html#subprocess.check_output</a>) returns a byte sequence; consider converting it to a string ("utf-8" encoding) and splitting it by newline (<code>\n</code>) to get a list. This will be useful for answering following questions.</p>
<p dir="auto"><strong>Important:</strong> if you use "git checkout" commands (as required for following exercises) to switch the current (HEAD) commit, the output of "git log" will change (it will show the current commit and prior ones, but not future commits).  You may want to add some code that runs "git checkout main" to bring the HEAD back to the latest commit.  Otherwise your answer to this question might only be correct the first time you run the notebook.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q26-what-are-the-commit-numbers-of-the-30-earliest-commits" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q26-what-are-the-commit-numbers-of-the-30-earliest-commits"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q26: What are the commit numbers of the 30 earliest commits?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Answer with a list.  Commits with earlier dates should be later in the list.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q27-what-did-the-readme-file-contain-after-the-2nd-commit" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q27-what-did-the-readme-file-contain-after-the-2nd-commit"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q27: what did the <code>README</code> file contain after the 2nd commit?</h3>
<p dir="auto">Use <code>check_output</code> to run a <code>git checkout</code> command to switch to that commit, before reading <code>flask/README</code> the way you would read any regular text file in Python (using <code>open</code> and <code>.read</code>).</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q28-how-many-pull-requests-were-merged-from-each-github-user--only-output-users-who-appear-5-or-more-times" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q28-how-many-pull-requests-were-merged-from-each-github-user--only-output-users-who-appear-5-or-more-times"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q28: how many pull requests were merged from each GitHub user?  Only output users who appear 5 or more times.</h3>
<p dir="auto">When running <code>git log</code>, you'll see some entries like this:</p>

<p dir="auto">Whenever a line from <code>git log</code> contains the text "Merge pull request" and "/", extract the username immediately before the "/".  Count occurences of usernames in dictionary like the following:</p>

<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q29-what-is-the-output-of-pip3-instal--yes-the-mispelling-was-intentional" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q29-what-is-the-output-of-pip3-instal--yes-the-mispelling-was-intentional"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q29: what is the output of <code>pip3 instal</code>?  (yes, the mispelling was intentional)</h3>
<p dir="auto">This one will be difficult because the command will fail, triggering
an exception.  First, run this by itself to determine what exception
type is thrown in this circumstance:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="check_output([&quot;pip3&quot;, &quot;instal&quot;])"><pre><span class="pl-en">check_output</span>([<span class="pl-s">"pip3"</span>, <span class="pl-s">"instal"</span>])</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Now, adapt this code to get the output, regardless of whether the
program runs or fails:</p>
<p dir="auto">Search the page here to learn about the exception type, and import it: <a href="https://docs.python.org/3/library/subprocess.html" rel="nofollow">https://docs.python.org/3/library/subprocess.html</a></p>
<p dir="auto">Then, use that information to catch exceptions of that type (fill in the missing exception type):</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="import subprocess

try:
    msg = check_output([&quot;pip3&quot;, &quot;instal&quot;], stderr=subprocess.STDOUT)
except subprocess.???? as e:
    msg = e.output"><pre><span class="pl-k">import</span> <span class="pl-s1">subprocess</span>

<span class="pl-k">try</span>:
    <span class="pl-s1">msg</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-en">check_output</span>([<span class="pl-s">"pip3"</span>, <span class="pl-s">"instal"</span>], <span class="pl-s1">stderr</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span><span class="pl-s1">subprocess</span>.<span class="pl-v">STDOUT</span>)
<span class="pl-k">except</span> <span class="pl-s1">subprocess</span>.???? <span class="pl-k">as</span> <span class="pl-s1">e</span>:
    <span class="pl-s1">msg</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s1">e</span>.<span class="pl-s1">output</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Note that <code>stderr=subprocess.STDOUT</code> tells <code>check_output</code> that error
messages should be treated as regular output -- that let's us capture
the output with <code>e.output</code>.</p>
<h3 tabindex="-1" dir="auto"><a id="user-content-q30-what-is-faster-for-looping-over-a-dataframe-iterrows-or-itertuples" class="anchor" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1" href="#q30-what-is-faster-for-looping-over-a-dataframe-iterrows-or-itertuples"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a>Q30: what is faster for looping over a DataFrame, <code>iterrows</code> or <code>itertuples</code>?</h3>
<p dir="auto">We'll want to generate test data of various sizes.  Use this function for that purpose:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def rand_df(rows):
    return pd.DataFrame(np.random.randint(10, size=(rows, 4)),
                        columns=[&quot;A&quot;, &quot;B&quot;, &quot;C&quot;, &quot;D&quot;],
                        index=[f&quot;r{i}&quot; for i in range(1, rows+1)])"><pre><span class="pl-k">def</span> <span class="pl-en">rand_df</span>(<span class="pl-s1">rows</span>):
    <span class="pl-k">return</span> <span class="pl-s1">pd</span>.<span class="pl-v">DataFrame</span>(<span class="pl-s1">np</span>.<span class="pl-s1">random</span>.<span class="pl-en">randint</span>(<span class="pl-c1">10</span>, <span class="pl-s1">size</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span>(<span class="pl-s1">rows</span>, <span class="pl-c1">4</span>)),
                        <span class="pl-s1">columns</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span>[<span class="pl-s">"A"</span>, <span class="pl-s">"B"</span>, <span class="pl-s">"C"</span>, <span class="pl-s">"D"</span>],
                        <span class="pl-s1">index</span><span class="pl-c1">=</span>[<span class="pl-s">f"r<span class="pl-s1"><span class="pl-kos">{</span><span class="pl-s1">i</span><span class="pl-kos">}</span></span>"</span> <span class="pl-k">for</span> <span class="pl-s1">i</span> <span class="pl-c1">in</span> <span class="pl-en">range</span>(<span class="pl-c1">1</span>, <span class="pl-s1">rows</span><span class="pl-c1">+</span><span class="pl-c1">1</span>)])</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Answer with a plot as follows:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>x-axis is number of number of rows in a DataFrame</li>
<li>y-axis is milliseconds is how long it takes to loop over the DataFrame</li>
<li>two lines: one for <code>iterrows</code> and one for <code>itertuples</code></li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">If you have a DataFrame generated from <code>rand_df</code> called <code>df</code>, you can take a measurement like this:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="t0 = time()
# HOW LONG DOES THIS TAKE TO RUN, FOR DATAFRAMES WITH DIFFERENT NUMBERS OF ROWS?
for row in df.iterrows():
    pass
t1 = time()"><pre><span class="pl-s1">t0</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-en">time</span>()
<span class="pl-c"># HOW LONG DOES THIS TAKE TO RUN, FOR DATAFRAMES WITH DIFFERENT NUMBERS OF ROWS?</span>
<span class="pl-k">for</span> <span class="pl-s1">row</span> <span class="pl-c1">in</span> <span class="pl-s1">df</span>.<span class="pl-en">iterrows</span>():
    <span class="pl-k">pass</span>
<span class="pl-s1">t1</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-en">time</span>()</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Your plot should look something like this (we're hiding the legend labels so it's a surprise for you which is faster). The labels of x-axis and y-axis should be as shown in the below graph.</p>
<p dir="auto"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="/msyamkumar/cs320-s23-projects/blob/main/p1/img/q30.png"><img src="/msyamkumar/cs320-s23-projects/raw/main/p1/img/q30.png" style="max-width: 100%;"></a></p>
<p dir="auto">Some noise is OK as long as you get the same general shape (we get a slightly different plot each time we measure ourselves).</p>
<p dir="auto">The easiest way to create a plot with two lines is to create a DataFrame with a column of measurements corresponding to each line.  Here's a simple example to adapt:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="times_df = pd.DataFrame(dtype=float)
times_df.at[1, &quot;A&quot;] = 50
times_df.at[2, &quot;A&quot;] = 60
times_df.at[1, &quot;B&quot;] = 35

<span class="pl-s1">times_df</span>.<span class="pl-s1">at</span>[<span class="pl-c1">1</span>, <span class="pl-s">"A"</span>] <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">50</span>
<span class="pl-s1">times_df</span>.<span class="pl-s1">at</span>[<span class="pl-c1">2</span>, <span class="pl-s">"A"</span>] <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">60</span>
<span class="pl-s1">times_df</span>.<span class="pl-s1">at</span>[<span class="pl-c1">1</span>, <span class="pl-s">"B"</span>] <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">35</span>
<span class="pl-s1">times_df</span>.<span class="pl-s1">at</span>[<span class="pl-c1">2</span>, <span class="pl-s">"B"</span>] <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">34</span>
